The Effect of Hayd and Nifas on Prayer and Fasting: An Usul Analysis
Explaining the distinction between qiyas and nass in deriving rulings on menstruation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school differentiates between qada of missed prayers and missed fasts during menstruation, by identifying the roles of qiyas and nass in deriving the ruling.

Core Idea
Purity is condition for prayer by qiyas; for fasting it is by nass, so qada differs.

In this passage, the author unpacks a key usul distinction: how textual evidence (nass) can override analogical reasoning (qiyas). The ruling on menstruation's effect on prayer and fasting serves as a concrete example. We will trace how the Hanafi jurists derived the rule that women make up missed fasts but not prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage states that menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) do not negate legal capacity (ahliyyah). Despite this, prayer and fasting are not performed during these states. Note this apparent paradox.
Ahliyyah remains intact, so the prohibition must come from another factor.
2
Examine the role of taharah
For prayer, ritual purity (taharah) is a condition. Its absence prevents valid performance. The author notes that this is consistent with qiyas (analogical reasoning) because purity is logically required for prayer.
Qiyas aligns with the textual ruling here.
3
Understand the nass-qiyas conflict for fasting
For fasting, the condition of purity is established by explicit text (nass), contrary to qiyas. Normally, fasting is valid even without purity (e.g., in janabah), so without the nass, fasting would be permitted during hayd. The nass overrides qiyas.
This is a clear example where nass takes precedence over qiyas.
4
Trace the qada distinction
Because the purity condition for prayer is based on qiyas, the obligation to make up (qada) does not extend to it. For fasting, the condition is based on nass, which also establishes the obligation of qada. The passage notes there is no hardship in making up fasts.
The difference in the source of the condition (qiyas vs nass) leads to different qada rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحيض والنفاس (۹) اور حیض (۱۰) اور نفاس اس کا عطفہ بھی ماقبل پر ہے یعنی صفر پر ہے ان دونوں و مصنف نے مرض کے بعد ہی ذکر کیا ہے کیونکہ یہ دونوں چیزیں بھی مرض سے متعلق ہیں کیونکہ یہ دونوں بھی مرض کی طرح اعزار میں سے ہیں۔
وَهُمَا لأَيُعْدِمَانِ الأهلية ان دونوں کی وجہ سے اہلیت زائل نہیں ہوتی ہے نہ اہلیت وجوب زائل ہوتی ہے نہ اہلیت اداء زائل ہوتی ہے فکان ینبغی لہذا مناسب تھا کہ ان دونوں اعزاز کی وجہ سے ان دونوں سے نماز اور روزہ ساقط نہ ہوتے لكن الطهارة لِلصَّلوة شرْطُ فِي قَوْتِ الشَّرْطِ فَوْتَ الأَدَاءِ لكن طہارت شرط ہے نماز کے لئے اور شرط کے مفقود ہونے سے اداء کا فوت ہونا لازمی ہے اس حد تک تو نص کے ساتھ قیاس کی موافقت پائی جاتی ہے۔
وَقَدْ جَعَلَتِ الطَّهَارَةُ عَنْهُما شرطًا لصحة الصومِ نَصًا بِخِلَافِ الْقِيَاسِ إِذَ الصوم يتَادى بِالْحَدَثِ وَلْجَنَابَةِ فِيَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَتَادَى بِالْحَيْضِ وَالنَّفَاسِ لَوْلاً النَّص وَقَدْ تَقَرّرَ مِنْ هَمَنَا أَن لا تُؤدى الصلوة والصومُ فِي حَالَةِ الْحَيْضِ وَالنَّقَاسِ فَإِذَن لأبُدْ أَنْ يُفَرِّقَ بَيْنَ قَضَائِهِمَا وَهُوَ أَن شَرْطَ الطَّهَارَةِ فِيْهِ خِلافُ الْقَيَاسِ فَلَمْ يَتَعَدُّ إِلَى الْقَضَاءِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَأَحَرَجَ فِي قَضَائِهِ إِذْ قَصَاءُ صَوْمٍ عَشِرَةٍ
And menstruation and postpartum bleeding (are mentioned). These are conjuncted to what preceded, i.e., upon illness, because these two are also excuses. The author mentioned them after illness because they are related to it. These two do not negate capacity (ahliyyah) – neither capacity of obligation nor capacity of performance – so it would be appropriate that prayer and fasting not be dropped due to them. But purification (taharah) is a condition for prayer, and the loss of the condition necessitates the loss of performance. To this extent, the qiyas agrees with the nass. And the nass has made purification from these two a condition for the validity of fasting, contrary to qiyas because fasting can be performed with minor impurity (hadath) and major impurity (janabah), so it would be appropriate that it be performed with menstruation and postpartum bleeding were it not for the nass. It is established from here that prayer and fasting are not performed in the state of menstruation and postpartum bleeding; thus it is necessary to differentiate between their qada (making up). That is: the condition of purification in it is contrary to qiyas, so it does not extend to qada, although there is no difficulty in qada because the qada of fasting ten days... (text cuts off?)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal effect of menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) on the obligation of prayer and fasting. The author explains that these conditions do not remove a woman's capacity (ahliyyah) for either obligation or performance. Despite this, prayer is not performed because ritual purity (taharah) is a prerequisite, and the absence of purity prevents valid performance. This ruling is consistent with analogical reasoning (qiyas). However, fasting is also not performed during these states, but here the ruling is based on explicit textual evidence (nass) that purity from these is a condition for the validity of fasting, which is contrary to qiyas because fasting is normally valid even in a state of minor or major impurity. The passage then notes a distinction regarding makeup (qada): women must make up missed fasts but not missed prayers. This is because the condition of purity for prayer is a logical requirement, so its absence excuses performance but does not necessitate makeup; for fasting, the nass requires purity, so the obligation of makeup is established by nass despite the qiyas being otherwise. The commentary clarifies that there is no hardship in making up fasts.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstruation, a natural monthly discharge of blood from a woman's uterus.
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth, similar to hayd in legal rulings.
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity: ability to acquire rights and perform duties.
القضاء (al-qada): Making up an obligatory act after its time has passed.
الطهارة (al-taharah): Ritual purity (wudu or ghusl) required for certain acts of worship.
نص (nass): An explicit legal text from the Quran or sunnah that is clear and decisive in meaning.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do women need to make up missed fasts due to menstruation but not missed prayers?
A) Because fasting is physically easier than prayer.
B) Because the condition of purity for prayer is analogical (qiyas), while for fasting it is textual (nass).
C) Because menstruation negates capacity for prayer but not for fasting.
D) Because the Quran explicitly says to make up prayers but not fasts.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between nass and qiyas determines whether qada is obligatory: only nass-based conditions extend to makeup, not qiyas-based ones.

Freeing a Slave with Attached Rights: The Hanafi Distinction between Yad and Raqabah
Ruling with explanation and comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a right attached to possession (yad) does not block the validity of a transaction, while a right attached to the essence (raqabah) may suspend it, by analyzing the cases of a sick person's slave and a pawned slave.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave is suspended when creditors' rights attach to his essence, but immediate when only a possession right attaches.

This passage addresses a nuanced fiqh scenario: a person on their deathbed frees a slave while debts are outstanding. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between rights attached to the slave's essence and rights attached only to possession. The author uses a comparison with a pawned slave to clarify the underlying usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios and rulings
Recognize the two cases: (A) sick person frees a slave with debts or high value → freeing suspended until death; (B) pawner frees a pawned slave → freeing immediate. Note the exception in (A) if the sick person has other wealth.
2
Identify the attached rights
In (A), creditors and heirs have rights to the slave's value (essence). In (B), the pawnholder has a right to possession only. This difference is the key.
3
Extract the usul principle
The distinction is between haqq al-raqaba (right over essence) and haqq al-yad (right over possession). When the right is over essence, the owner's disposition is suspended; when over possession, it is valid immediately.
This principle explains why the rulings differ.
4
Apply the principle to explain the exception
In (A), if the sick person has other wealth, the creditors' right no longer attaches to the slave's essence (since there are other assets), so the freeing becomes immediate. This confirms the principle.
5
Generalize the rule
Formulate a broader usul rule: any disposition by an owner that conflicts with a right attached to the essence of the property is suspended until that right is satisfied; but a disposition conflicting with a right attached to possession alone is valid and takes effect immediately.
Apply this to other areas like mortgage and sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سے غلام آزاد کر دے جو مال کی قرض میں متفرق ہے قرض میں پھنسا ہوا ہے یا ایسے غلام کو آزاد کر دے جس کی قیمت ایک ٹمٹ سے زائد ہو تو مریض کی موت واقع ہونے تک یہ غلام مدیر کے حکم میں ہو گا ان تمام اعزازی احکام سے محروم رہنے میں جو آزاد کے ساتھ خاص ہوتے ہیں اور مریض کے انتقال ہونے کے بعد وہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اپنی قیمت کما کر قرض خواہوں اور ورثہ کو دے گا اور اگر مریض کے پاس علاوہ غلام کے مال اتنا ہے کہ قرض پو را ادا ہو سکتا ہے یا غلام کی قیمت ممث مال سے کم کم ہے تو حق فی الحال نافذ ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اس غلام کے ساتھ کسی کا حق متعلق نہیں ہے۔ بخلاف إعتاق الزامن حنث تند لیکن راہن کے غلام کو آزاد کر دینے کا حکم اس سے مختلف ہے کہ اس کا تصرف فی الحال نافذ ہو جاتا ہے مصنف کی یہ عبارت ایک مقدر سوال کا جواب ہے کہ تم کہتے ہو کہ عشق فی الحال نافذ نہیں ہوتا جبکہ غلام پر (عزیم) قرض خواہ اور وراٹ کا حق متعلق ہے اس کے باوجو د را من کے آزاد کر دینے سے حق کو نافذ قرار دیتے ہو حالانکہ یہ غلام وہ ہے جس کے ساتھ سر تہن کا حق متعلق ہے تو مصنف نے جواب دیا کہ راہن کا آزاد کر دینا اس وجہ سے نافذ قرار دیا گیا ہے کیونکہ لان حق المرتهن في اليد دون الرقبة كيونکہ غلام مرتہن کا محض حق قبضہ ہے غلام کے رقبہ (یعنی مالیست) کے ساتھ اس کا حق متعلق نہیں ہے اس وجہ سے کہ راہن کا حق غلام پر بہاتی ہے اور احتراق کا صحیح ہونا اس پر موقوف ہوتا ہے۔
(The sick person) frees a slave who is entangled in debt, or frees a slave whose value exceeds one mint, then this slave will remain under the manager's ruling until the patient's death, deprived of all honorary rulings specific to free persons. After the patient's death, the slave becomes free and will earn his value to pay creditors and heirs. If the patient has enough wealth besides the slave to repay the debt, or the slave's value is less than that wealth, then the right takes effect immediately because no one's right is attached to this slave. Contrary to the freeing of a pawned slave: the ruling is different because his transaction takes effect immediately. The author's expression answers an implied question: you say that freeing does not take effect immediately when creditors' and heirs' rights are attached to the slave, yet you declare the freeing of a pawned slave effective even though the pawnholder's right is attached. The author replies: the freeing of a pawned slave is deemed effective because the pawnholder's right is only over possession (yad), not over the slave's essence (raqabah); the pawnholder has no right over the slave's corpus.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling and reasoning about freeing a slave in the context of the sick person's deathbed (marad al-mawt) and the pawned slave (rahn). The text first states the default ruling: if a sick person frees a slave who is in debt or whose value exceeds one mint (possibly a currency unit), the freeing is suspended until death, and the slave remains under the manager's control, not enjoying the rights of free persons. After death, the slave is freed and must work to repay creditors and heirs. However, if the patient has other sufficient wealth to cover debts or the slave's value is less, the freeing takes effect immediately because no one else has a claim on that slave. The author then clarifies a possible confusion: why does the freeing of a pawned slave (by the pawner?) take effect immediately despite the pawnholder's right? Because the pawnholder's right is only over possession (yad), not over the slave's essence (raqabah). Thus, the slave's corporeal property is free from the pawnholder's claim, and the freeing is valid. This distinction is rooted in the Hanafi understanding of rights attached to property.

Key Terms
رقبہ (raqabah): The corporeal substance or essence of a slave, as opposed to usufruct or possession rights.
يد (yad): Possession or control, as opposed to ownership of the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the freeing of a pawned slave take effect immediately according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the pawnholder's right is over the slave's essence (raqabah)
B) Because the pawnholder's right is over possession (yad) only, not the slave's essence
C) Because the pawnholder has no right at all
D) Because the sick person's freeing is always immediate

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between rights attached to the essence (raqabah) and rights attached to possession (yad); this distinction determines whether an owner's disposition is suspended or immediate.

Menstruation, Postpartum, and Their Effects on Worship: A Usul al-Fiqh Analysis
Explains menstruation and postpartum as excuses, their effect on ahliyyah, and the distinction between qada of prayer and fasting based on nass versus qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why menstruation and postpartum do not remove legal capacity (ahliyyah) and apply the Hanafi usul principle that a text-based condition (nass) contrary to qiyas does not extend to qada, using the distinction between prayer and fasting.

Core Idea
Menstruation and postpartum do not remove ahliyyah; the text-based condition for fasting does not extend to qada, but prayer qada is excused due to hardship.

This passage addresses a common confusion: if menstruation and postpartum are excuses ('udhr), why are missed fasts required to be made up but missed prayers not? The answer lies in the nature of the condition of purification: for prayer it is agreed upon by text and reason, but for fasting it is imposed by text (nass) against analogy (qiyas). This text-based exception does not carry over to qada, and qada of prayer would cause hardship, so it is waived. The passage then extends to death, where all acts of worship requiring intention drop from the deceased, refuting the Shafi'i view on zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on ahliyyah
State that menstruation and postpartum do not remove ahliyyah (legal capacity) – neither for obligation (wujub) nor for performance (ada'). They are excuses ('udhr) like illness.
Ahliyyah is only removed by death or insanity.
2
Analyze the condition of purification for prayer and fasting
Purification is a condition for prayer and fasting. For prayer, loss of condition naturally prevents performance (qiyas). For fasting, the condition is established by text (nass) contrary to qiyas, because fasting is valid with minor/major impurity.
The text overrides analogy, showing the authority of nass over qiyas.
3
Distinguish qada of prayer and fasting
The text-based condition for fasting does not extend to qada, so qada of fasting is required with no hardship. Qada of prayer would involve hardship (qada of ten days' prayers in twenty days), so it is excused.
This distinction relies on the principle that exceptional rulings (contrary to qiyas) are not extended by analogy.
4
Examine the ruling on death and acts of worship
Death removes ahliyyah for all acts of worship requiring intention (including zakat). The Hanafi view: such worships cannot be performed by proxy after death, countering the Shafi'i claim that zakat can be paid by the guardian.
This is based on the illa that these worships require choice and performance, not merely wealth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحيض والنفاس (۹) اور حیض (۱۰) اور نفاس اس کا عطفہ بھی ماقبل پر ہے یعنی صفر پر ہے ان دونوں و مصنف نے مرض کے بعد ہی ذکر کیا ہے کیونکہ یہ دونوں چیزیں بھی مرض سے متعلق ہیں کیونکہ یہ دونوں بھی مرض کی طرح اعزار میں سے ہیں۔ وَهُمَا لأَيُعْدِمَانِ الأهلية ان دونوں کی وجہ سے اہلیت زائل نہیں ہوتی ہے نہ اہلیت وجوب زائل ہوتی ہے نہ اہلیت اداء زائل ہوتی ہے فکان ینبغی لہذا مناسب تھا کہ ان دونوں اعزاز کی وجہ سے ان دونوں سے نماز اور ور زو ساقط نہ ہوتے لكن المبَارَةَ لِلصَّلوة شرْطُ فِي قَوْتِ الشَّرْطِ فَوْتَ الأَدَاءِ لكن طہارت شرط ہے نماز کے لئے اور شرط کے مفقود ہونے سے اداء کا فوت ہونا لازمی ہے اس حد تک تو نص کے ساتھ قیاس کی موافقت پائی جاتی ہے۔ وَقَدْ جَعَلَتِ الطَّهَارَةُ عَنْهُما شرطًا لصحة الصومِ نَصَا بِخِلَافِ الْقِيَاسِ إِذَ الصوم يتَادى بِالْحَدَثِ وَلْجَنَابَةِ فِيَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَتَادَى بِالْحَيْضِ وَالنَّفَاسِ لَوْلاً النَّص وَقَدْ تَقَرّرَ مِنْ هَمَنَا أَن لا تُؤدى الصلوة والصومُ فِي حَالَةِ الْحَيْضِ وَالنَّقَاسِ فَإِذَن لأبُدْ أَنْ يُفَرِّقَ بَيْنَ قَضَائِهِمَا وَهُوَ أَن شَرْطَ الطَّهَارَةِ فِيْهِ خِلافُ الْقَيَاسِ فَلَمْ يَتَعَدُّ إِلَى الْقَضَاءِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَأَحَرَجَ فِي قَضَائِهِ إِذْ قَصَاءُ صَوْمٍ عَشِرَةٍ أَيَّامٍ فِي مَابَيْنَ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ شَيْرًا مِمَّا لا يَضَيْقُ وَإِنْ فُرِضَ أَنْ يَسْتَوْعِبَ النَّفَاسُ شهْرَ رَمَضَانَ كَامِلَةٌ مِمْعَ أَنَّهُ نَادِرٌ لأَنْبَاطِ بِهِ أَحْكَامُ الشرْعِ أَيْضًا لأَحَرَجَ فَيْهِ إذْ قَضاهُ صَوم شهر وَاحِدٍ فِى أَحَدَ عَشَرَ شَهْرًا مِمَّا حَرَج فيه بخلاف المثلوة فِيْهِ فَإِنْ فِي قَضاءِ صَلوَةٍ عَشْرَةَ أَيَّامٍ فِى كُلِّ عِشْرِينَ يَوْمًا مِمَّا يُقْضِي إِلَى الْحَرِج غَالِبًا فَإِذَا تُعْفَى وَالْمَوْتُ عَطَفْ عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ وَهُوَ آخِرُ الأُمُورِ الْمُعْتَرِضَةِ السَّمَاوِيَةِ وَإِنَّهُ يُنَافِى الأَهْلِيَةِ فِى أحكام الدُّنْيَا مِمَّا فِيْهِ تَكْلِينَ حَتَّى يَطْلِت الزَّكَوَةُ وَسَائِرُ الْقُرْبِ عَنْهُ وَإِنَّمَا خُص الزَّكَوةُ أَوْلاً دَفْعًا لِوَهُم مَنْ يَتُوهُمُ أَنَّهَا عِبَادَةٌ مَالِيَةٌ لاَتَعَلَّقُ بِفِعْلِ الْمَيِّتِ فَيُؤْذَيْهَا الْوَلِيُّ كمَا زَعَمَ السَّافَعِي وَذَلِكَ لإنَّهُمَا عِبَادَةٌ لاَبُدَّلَهَا مِنَ الاِخْتِيارِ وَالمَقْصُودُ مِنْهَا الآدَاءُ دُونَ المَالِ فِى تُساوئ الصلوة والصَّوْمَ فِي الْبُطْلَانِ وَإِنْمَا يَبْقِي عَلَيْهِ الْمَاثَمُ لأَغَيْرَ فَإِنْ شَاءَ الله عَفَا عَنْهُ بَفَضْلِهِ وَكَرَمِهِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ عَذِّبَهُ بِعَدْلِهِ وَحِكْمَتِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ حَالَ حَقٌّ الله تَعَالَى وَأمَّا حَقَّ الْعِبَادِ فَلا يَخْلُوْ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَقًّا لِلْغَيْر عَلَيْهِ أَوْ حَقًّا لَهُ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ وَأَشَارَ إِلى الأَوَّلِ بَقَوْلِهِ وَمَا سُرِعَ عَلَيْهِ لِحَاجَةِ غَيْرِهِ فَإِنْ كَانَ حَقًّا مُتَعَلِّقًا بِالْعَيْنِ يَبْقَى بِنِقَائِهِ كَالْمَرْمُون يَتَعَلْقُ بِهِ حَق الْمُرَتَيْنِ الْمُساجِرِ يَتَعَلَّقُ حَقُّ الْمُسْتَاجِرِ وَالْمَبِيْعِ يَتَعَلَّقَ بِه حَقَّ الْمُسْتَرِئُ وَالْوَدِيْعَةُ يَتَعَلَّقَ بِهَا حَقٌّ الْمُودَع فَإِنَّ هَذِ الأَعْيَانَ يَاخُذُ مَا صَاحِبُ الْحَق أَوْلاً مِنْ غَيْرِ إِنْ تَدْخُل فِي التركَةِ وَتَقْسَمُ عَلى الغُرَمَاءِ أو الورثة.
And menstruation and postpartum bleeding: these two are also items of excuse (a'dhar) like illness. These two do not remove eligibility (ahliyyah) – neither wajub nor ada'. So it would be appropriate that prayer and fasting are not dropped due to these two excuses. But purification is a condition for prayer, and the loss of the condition necessitates loss of performance; to this extent analogy agrees with the text. And purification from these two has been made a condition for the validity of fasting by text, contrary to analogy, because fasting can be performed with minor impurity (hadath) and major impurity (janabah). So it would be expected that it be performed during menstruation and postpartum if not for the text. From here it is established that prayer and fasting are not performed during menstruation and postpartum. So a distinction must be made in their qada: the condition of purification in it is contrary to analogy, so it does not extend to qada, especially since there is no hardship in qada of fasting – the qada of ten days of fasting in the span of eleven months is not constricting. Even if postpartum bleeding occupies the entire month of Ramadan, which is rare, the rulings of Sharia apply; there is no hardship in it because the qada of one month's fast in eleven months is not burdensome. Unlike prayer, because the qada of ten days of prayer in every twenty days generally leads to hardship, so it is excused. And death is joined to what precedes, and it is the last of the heavenly obstructing matters; it removes eligibility in worldly rulings involving obligation, to the extent that zakat and all other acts of worship drop from him. Zakat is specifically mentioned first to refute the misunderstanding of those who think it is a financial worship not dependent on the deceased's action, so the guardian performs it as Shafi'i claimed. That is because both are worship that require choice and their purpose is performance, not wealth, so they are like prayer and fasting in being invalid. Only sin remains on him, and if Allah wills, He forgives by His grace, or punishes by His justice. This is the case with rights of Allah. As for rights of servants, they are either a right owed to him or a right he owes. He pointed to the first by saying 'and what is taken from him due to another's need.' If it is a right attached to a specific item, it remains with its removal, like the pawned property to which the pawnholder's right attaches, the rental property to which the lessee's right attaches, the sold item to which the buyer's right attaches, and the deposit to which the depositor's right attaches. Indeed, these items are taken by the right-holder first without entering the estate and being divided among creditors or heirs.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi commentary explaining the rulings of menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) and their impact on eligibility (ahliyyah) and worship. The text first states that these are like illness – they are excuses (a'dhar) that do not remove eligibility for obligations (wujub) or performance (ada'). So logically, prayer and fasting should not be dropped, but purification is a condition for prayer, so its absence prevents performance. For fasting, the text (nass) made purification from these a condition, contrary to analogy (qiyas), because fasting is valid with minor or major impurity. The text then distinguishes between qada of fasting and prayer: since the condition is text-based, it does not extend to qada, and qada of fasting is easy (ten days' fast in eleven months), while qada of prayer would require hardship (twenty days' prayer in forty days), so prayer's qada is excused. The passage then transitions to death: death removes eligibility for all worldly obligations involving taklif; zakat and other acts of worship fall. The text specifically mentions zakat to counter the Shafi'i view that zakat, being financial worship, can be performed by the guardian on behalf of the deceased. The Hanafi position is that all worship requires intention and choice, so if not performed during life, they cannot be made up after death; only sin remains. Finally, the text discusses rights of servants: if a right is attached to a specific physical item (like pawn, rent, sale, deposit), that item is given to the right-holder before entering the estate.

Key Terms
اہلیت (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to acquire rights (ahliyyat wujub) and to perform acts (ahliyyat ada').
نص (nass): A clear textual ruling from Quran or Sunnah that is explicit and unambiguous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, why is qada of fasting required for a woman who misses fasts due to menstruation but qada of prayer is not required?
A) Because fasting is more important than prayer
B) Because the condition of purification for prayer is based on analogy, while for fasting it is based on text
C) Because the condition of purification for fasting is based on text contrary to analogy, so it does not extend to qada, and qada of fasting is not burdensome
D) Because prayer cannot be made up at all

Key Takeaway
The text-based condition for fasting is an exception that does not extend to qada, while the condition for prayer is based on both text and analogy, and its qada is excused due to hardship; death removes ahliyyah for all acts of worship requiring intention.

Exception of Qada Fasting from Purification Condition: Hanafi Usul Reasoning
Explaining why purification from hayd/nifas is not required for qada of fasting due to lack of hardship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school exempts qada fasting from the purification condition using the principle of lack of hardship (haraj) and contrast it with prayer qada.

Core Idea
Absence of hardship (haraj) exempts qada fasting from the purification condition, unlike prayer qada.

This passage addresses a key nuance in Hanafi usul: the purification condition for fasting, established by nass contrary to qiyas, applies only to the original performance (ada), not to the makeup (qada). The reasoning hinges on the concept of hardship (haraj). We will dissect why fasting qada incurs no hardship while prayer qada does, and how this distinction preserves the spirit of the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling on purification
The Hanafi school holds that purification from hayd/nifas is a condition for the validity of fasting based on nass (textual evidence) even though qiyas would suggest otherwise.
This condition is specific to the original fasts (ada).
2
Distinguish ada from qada
The passage clarifies that the condition does not extend to qada (makeup) fasting because requiring purification for makeup would impose hardship.
Qada fasts can be performed over eleven months, offering flexibility.
3
Apply the talil: comparison with prayer qada
The author contrasts fasting qada with prayer qada: praying makeup prayers every twenty days leads to continuous hardship, so the purification condition is required for prayer qada. For fasting, the long time frame eliminates hardship.
This contrast shows the role of haraj in determining exceptions.
4
Derive the usul principle
The rule is: a condition established by nass contrary to qiyas applies only where its application does not cause undue hardship. Hardship (haraj) is a valid ground for limiting its scope.
This is a form of takhsis bil-urf or istihsan based on maslahah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَيَّامٍ فِي مَا بَيْنَ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ شَهْرًا مِمَّا لا يَضِيقُ وَإِنْ فُرِضَ أَنْ يَسْتَوْعِبَ النَّفَاسُ شَهْرَ رَمَضَانَ كَامِلَةً مَعَ أَنَّهُ نَادِرٌ لا يُبْنَى بِهِ أَحْكَامُ الشَّرْعِ أَيْضًا لا حَرَجَ فِيهِ إِذْ قَضَاهُ صَوْمَ شَهْرٍ وَاحِدٍ فِي أَحَدَ عَشَرَ شَهْرًا مِمَّا لا حَرَجَ فِيهِ بِخِلَافِ الْمَثْلُوۃِ [؟] فِيهِ فَإِنَّ فِي قَضَاءِ صَلَاةِ عَشَرَةِ أَيَّامٍ فِي كُلِّ عِشْرِينَ يَوْمًا مِمَّا يُفْضِي إِلَى الْحَرَجِ غَالِبًا فَإِذَا تُعْفَى
Days in a period of eleven months without hardship. And if it is assumed that postpartum bleeding covers the entire month of Ramadan completely, even though it is rare and the rulings of Sharia are not built on it, there is no hardship in it since he makes up the fast of one month in eleven months, which has no hardship, unlike the similar [prayer] in it, for indeed in making up the prayer of ten days every twenty days, it generally leads to hardship, so it is excused.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling that purification from hayd and nifas is a condition for the validity of fasting based on nass, contrary to qiyas. This condition applies only to the original performance (ada), not to the makeup (qada), because there is no hardship in making up the fasts over eleven months. In contrast, making up missed prayers every twenty days would cause continuous hardship, so the condition is not extended to prayer qada. Thus, a woman makes up fasts without needing purification, but must be pure for prayer qada.

Key Terms
طهارة (tahara): Purification from hayd/nifas as a condition for fasting.
قضاء (qada): Making up missed obligatory acts of worship.
حرج (haraj): Hardship that leads to concession in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is purification from hayd/nifas a condition for the qada of fasting?
A) Yes, because fasting requires purification.
B) No, because the condition is only for the original fasts, not for qada due to lack of hardship.
C) Yes, because purification is required for all acts of worship.
D) No, because fasting is valid even with janaba.

Key Takeaway
The absence of hardship (haraj) in qada fasting is the basis for exempting it from the purification condition, illustrating the Hanafi use of istihsan.

Death Negates Legal Capacity for Worship Obligations
Explaining that death nullifies capacity for taklif, and zakat and other worship lapse, refuting Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that all acts of worship lapse upon death, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding financial worship using qiyas.

Core Idea
Death eliminates legal capacity; thus all obligatory acts of worship requiring choice cease, and no guardian can fulfill them.

This passage from Ashraf al-Anwaar addresses a fundamental usul principle: death terminates legal capacity (ahliyya) for all obligations involving taklif. The author specifically refutes the Shafi'i view that a guardian can pay zakat on behalf of the deceased, arguing that zakat, like prayer and fasting, is an act of worship requiring personal choice. By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi reasoning and the practical implications of this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the Hanafi rule that death negates legal capacity for all taklif-related obligations. The text explicitly says: 'death negates legal capacity... such that zakat and all acts of worship fall off from him.'
This applies to all acts of worship, not just zakat.
2
Understand the proof: qiyas to other acts of worship
The Hanafi proof is analogical: zakat is compared to prayer and fasting. All three require personal choice (ikhtiyar) and are acts of worship (ibadat). Therefore, they all become invalid (batil) upon death.
The illa (effective cause) is that worship requires personal performance; wealth is merely a medium.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argued that zakat is a financial obligation, so the wealth remains and the guardian can discharge it. The Hanafi response: the objective is performance (ada'), not wealth; worship requires choice.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, no zakat is paid from the estate of a deceased person who missed it. In the Shafi'i school, the guardian pays it. The same principle applies to other financial acts of worship like kaffarah (expiation) or sadaqat al-fitr.
Debts (rights of people) are not affected; they remain obligations on the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمَوْتُ عَطَفَ عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ وَهُوَ آخِرُ الْأُمُورِ الْمُعْتَرِضَةِ السَّمَاوِيَةِ وَإِنَّهُ يُنَافِي الْأَهْلِيَّةَ فِي أَحْكَامِ الدُّنْيَا مِمَّا فِيهِ تَكْلِيفٌ حَتَّى يَسْقُطَ الزَّكَاةُ وَسَائِرُ الْقُرَبِ عَنْهُ وَإِنَّمَا خُصَّ الزَّكَاةُ أَوَّلًا دَفْعًا لِوَهْمِ مَنْ يَتَوَهَّمُ أَنَّهَا عِبَادَةٌ مَالِيَّةٌ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِفِعْلِ الْمَيِّتِ فَيُؤَدِّيَهَا الْوَلِيُّ كَمَا زَعَمَ الشَّافِعِيُّ وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهَا عِبَادَةٌ لَا بُدَّ لَهَا مِنَ الِاخْتِيَارِ وَالْمَقْصُودُ مِنْهَا الْأَدَاءُ دُونَ الْمَالِ فَهِيَ تُسَاوِي الصَّلَاةَ وَالصَّوْمَ فِي الْبُطْلَانِ وَإِنَّمَا يَبْقَى عَلَيْهِ الْإِثْمُ لَا غَيْرُ فَإِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ عَفَا عَنْهُ بِفَضْلِهِ وَكَرَمِهِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ عَذَّبَهُ بِعَدْلِهِ وَحِكْمَتِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ حَالُ حَقِّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى
And death is conjoined to what preceded it, and it is the last of the celestial impediments. And it negates legal capacity in the rulings of the world that involve obligation, such that zakat and all acts of worship fall off from him. And zakat is specifically mentioned first to repel the misconception of one who imagines that it is a financial worship not attached to the action of the deceased, so the guardian performs it, as al-Shafi'i claimed. That is because it is an act of worship that necessarily requires choice, and the objective is performance, not wealth, so it is equal to prayer and fasting in invalidity. Only sin remains upon him, nothing else. Then if Allah wills, He pardons him by His grace and generosity, and if He wills, He punishes him by His justice and wisdom. This is the case of the right of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses death as the final celestial impediment that entirely removes legal capacity (ahliyya) for all obligations (taklif). Therefore, no act of worship, including zakat, can be performed on behalf of the deceased. Imam al-Shafi'i held that since zakat is a financial obligation, the guardian can pay it from the estate. The Hanafi response is that zakat is an act of worship requiring intention and choice; the wealth is only a medium. Hence it is like prayer and fasting. The deceased is only responsible for the sin of neglecting it, which is left to Allah's will.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyya): Legal capacity to bear rights and obligations.
تكليف (taklif): Legal obligation requiring intention and choice.
زكاة (zakat): Obligatory alms, an act of worship.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning, a source of law in usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does the Hanafi school permit the guardian to pay zakat on behalf of a deceased person?
A) Yes, because it is a financial obligation.
B) No, because zakat is an act of worship requiring choice.
C) Yes, if the deceased left a will.
D) No, only mandatory.

Key Takeaway
Death completely terminates legal capacity for all acts of worship requiring personal choice, so no guardian can fulfill them, and the deceased's sin is left to Allah's will.

Haqq al-Ayn vs Dayn After Death: Hanafi Usul Rule
Distinguishes between rights attached to specific objects and debts in inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqq al-ayn (right attached to a specific object) and dayn (debt) in the context of death, and state the Hanafi rule that haqq al-ayn survives death while dayn does not persist without property or guarantor.

Core Idea
A right attached to a specific object survives death; a pure debt perishes without property or guarantor.

In inheritance, not all claims against the deceased are equal. The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between rights tied to a physical object — like a mortgage, lease, or deposit — and mere debts. This distinction determines whether a claimant can recover directly from the estate before distribution or must wait for the Hereafter. This passage from Ashraf al-Anwar explains the rule with concrete examples and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of rights of people
The passage divides rights that people hold over the deceased into two: a right attached to a specific object (haqq al-ayn) and a debt (dayn). Recognize each type.
Haqq al-ayn examples: mortgage, lease, sale, deposit. Dayn: any financial obligation not tied to a specific object.
2
State the legal effect for haqq al-ayn
For haqq al-ayn, the right remains after death. The owner of the right takes the object directly, before the estate is divided among heirs or creditors.
The passage says: 'these objects are taken first without entering the estate.'
3
State the legal effect for dayn
A mere debt does not remain by the existence of the liability alone. It must be backed by property left by the deceased or by a guarantor (kafala). Otherwise, it is only recoverable in the Hereafter.
Abu Hanifa's ruling on suretyship for the deceased confirms this: no property or guarantor, no debt in this world.
4
Understand the ta'lil (rationale)
The debt does not persist because the liability (dhimmah) alone is insufficient; it requires an external support (mal or kafala). Haqq al-ayn is attached to a specific thing that remains after death.
This ta'lil is the basis for the Hanafi rule.
5
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Given a scenario, classify each claim as haqq al-ayn or dayn, and state whether it can be enforced immediately against the estate or only in the Hereafter.
Use the examples from the passage as templates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا حَقُّ الْعِبَادِ فَلَا يَخْلُو إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَقًّا لِلْغَيْرِ عَلَيْهِ أَوْ حَقًّا لَهُ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِقَوْلِهِ وَمَا شُرِعَ عَلَيْهِ لِحَاجَةِ غَيْرِهِ فَإِنْ كَانَ حَقًّا مُتَعَلِّقًا بِالْعَيْنِ يَبْقَى بِبَقَائِهِ كَالْمَرْهُونِ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ حَقُّ الْمُرْتَهِنِ وَالْمُسَاجِرِ يَتَعَلَّقُ حَقُّ الْمُسْتَأْجِرِ وَالْمَبِيعِ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ حَقُّ الْمُشْتَرِي وَالْوَدِيعَةِ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهَا حَقُّ الْمُودِعِ فَإِنَّ هَذِهِ الْأَعْيَانَ يَأْخُذُهَا صَاحِبُ الْحَقِّ أَوَّلًا مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ فِي التَّرِكَةِ وَتُقْسَمَ عَلَى الْغُرَمَاءِ أَوِ الْوَرَثَةِ. وَإِنْ كَانَ دَيْنًا لَمْ يَبْقَ بِمُجَرَّدِ الذِّمَّةِ حَتَّى يُضَمَّ إِلَيْهَا أَيْ إِلَى الذِّمَّةِ مَالٌ أَوْ مَا يُؤَكِّدُ بِهِ الذِّمَمُ وَهُوَ ذِمَّةُ الْكَفِيلِ يَعْنِي مَا لَمْ يَتْرُكْ مَالًا أَوْ كَفِيلًا مِنْ خُصُوصِهِ لَا يَبْقَى دَيْنُهُ فِي الدُّنْيَا فَلَا يُطَالِبُ بِهِ أَوْلَادَهُ وَإِنَّمَا يَأْخُذُهُ فِي الْآخِرَةِ وَلِهَذَا أَيْ لِأَجْلِ أَنَّهُ لَمْ يَبْقَ فِي ذِمَّتِهِ دَيْنٌ قَالَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ إِنَّ الْكَفَالَةَ بِالدَّيْنِ عَنِ الْمَيِّتِ
As for the rights of people, it is not free from either being a right of another upon him or a right of his upon another. He pointed to the first with his statement: 'And what is legislated upon him due to the need of another.' If it is a right attached to a specific object, it remains with its persistence, like the mortgaged item: the right of the mortgagee is attached to it; the rented item: the right of the lessee is attached; the sold item: the right of the buyer is attached; the deposit: the right of the depositor is attached. Indeed, the owner of the right takes these objects first without them entering into the estate and being divided among creditors or heirs. And if it is a debt, it does not remain by mere existence of the liability until there is property attached to it or something that strengthens the liability, which is the liability of a guarantor. Meaning, if he has not left property or a guarantor specifically, his debt does not remain in this world, so his children are not pursued for it; rather, he will take it in the Hereafter. For this reason, because no debt remained in his liability, Abu Hanifa said: 'Suretyship for the debt of a deceased person...'
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of rights people have over the deceased. First, a right attached to a specific object (haqq muta'alliq bi'l-ayn) such as a mortgage, lease, sale, or deposit. These rights survive death because the object itself is identifiable and the owner of the right can take it directly without the object entering the general estate. Second, a mere debt (dayn) without property or guarantor does not remain enforceable in this world; it is only recoverable in the Hereafter. Abu Hanifa's ruling on suretyship for a deceased person's debt confirms this: the debt only persists if there is property or a guarantor to support it.

Key Terms
حق العين (haqq al-ayn): A right attached to a specific physical object.
دين (dayn): A debt or financial obligation not attached to a specific object.
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship; guaranteeing a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a deceased person has mortgaged his house, can the mortgagee take the house before the estate is distributed?
A) No, the house goes to heirs.
B) Yes, because the right is attached to the object.
C) Only if the deceased owed other debts.
D) No, the mortgage is canceled at death.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, rights attached to specific objects survive death and are taken directly; mere debts do not persist without property or a guarantor.

6 min
Death as a Defensive Obstacle: Negation of Ahliyah for Huquq Allah
Explains death as a defensive obstacle negating legal capacity for huquq Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that death negates legal capacity for acts of worship, apply it to zakah, and explain the qiyas used to rebut the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Death fully removes eligibility for acts of worship; no one can fulfill them for the deceased.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, death is classified as a defensive obstacle ('arid sadi) that terminates legal capacity (ahliyah) for all worldly rulings, particularly rights of Allah (huquq Allah). This has a significant practical implication: obligations like zakah, prayer, and fasting cannot be performed on behalf of the deceased by a guardian. The author in this passage specifically singles out zakah to counter a common misconception that because zakah is financial, it can be paid after death. We will unpack the reasoning and the inter-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its category
State that death negates ahliyah for huquq Allah. Recognize that it is a defensive obstacle ('arid sadi).
This is the foundational legal principle.
2
Understand the scope of affected obligations
All acts of worship (prayer, fasting, zakah, etc.) are covered. The author lumps them together.
Huquq Allah are obligations toward God.
3
Analyze the special case of zakah
Zakah is mentioned first because it might be misunderstood as a financial right. The author clarifies that it is an act of worship.
Contrast with huquq al-ibad (e.g., debts).
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argued that zakah can be paid by a guardian because it is financial. The Hanafi response uses qiyas with prayer and fasting.
Focus on the illa (effective cause): acts of worship require volition.
5
Determine the consequence for the deceased
Only sin remains; no further obligation. The outcome depends on Allah's mercy or justice.
This is the practical eschatological effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموت (11) وأَنه ينافي الأهْلِيَةِ فِي أحكام الدُّنْيا المخ [؟] يہ ان احکام کی اہمیت کو ختم کر دیتا ہے جن کا وہ دنیا میں مکلف تھا چنانچہ زکوۃ اور دوسری عبادتیں اس کی طرف سے اداء کرنا باطل اور نا قابل اعتبار ہیں اور مصنف نے زکوۃ کو بطور خاص سب سے پہلے ذکر کیا ہے تاکہ اس وہم کو رفع کریں جو اس موقع پر کیا جاتاہے کہ زکوۃ چونکہ مالی عبادت ہے جو میت کے فعل سے متعلق نہیں ہے لہذا اس کو ولی اداء کر دے جیسا کہ امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے اور باطل ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ زکوۃ عبادت ہے اور اس میں اختیار کا ہونا ضروری ہے چونکہ اس سے مقصود ادائے گی ہے مال مقصود نہیں ہے اس لئے نماز روزہ کی طرح میت کی طرف سے ادائے گی کے باطل ہونے میں زکوۃ ان کے مساوی ہے۔ وَإِنَّمَا يَنْبَغِى عَلَيْهِ الْمَائِم [؟] ہاں اس کے حق میں صرف گناہ باقی رہ جائے گا اس کے علاوہ دوسری کوئی ذمہ داری نہ رہے گی پس اگر اللہ تعالی اپنے فضل و کرم سے چاہے تو اس سے اس کو معاف فرمادے اور اگر چاہے تو عذاب دے عدل اور حکمت کا تقاضہ یہی ہے یہ حقوق اللہ کا حال ہے
And death (11) and it negates eligibility in the rulings of this world... it ends the importance of those rulings for which he was obligated in the world. Thus zakah and other acts of worship performed on his behalf are invalid and unacceptable. And the author mentioned zakah specifically first to remove the misconception that since zakah is a financial worship not related to the deceased's action, the guardian can perform it, as Imam Shafi'i said. The reason for invalidity is that zakah is worship requiring volition, as its purpose is performance not wealth, so it is like prayer and fasting in being invalid when performed on behalf of the deceased. And only sin remains upon him; no other responsibility remains. Then if Allah wills, He may forgive him or punish him. This is the state of the rights of Allah.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage explains death as the last of the defensive obstacles ('awāriḍ sādi'ah) that remove legal capacity (ahliyah) for worldly rulings. The author first establishes that death negates eligibility for acts of worship (huquq Allah). Acts like zakah, prayer, and fasting performed on behalf of the deceased are invalid because they require the deceased's own intention and volition. The author specifically addresses a potential misconception: although zakah is a financial obligation, it is still an act of worship ('ibadah) and not merely a property right. Hence, it cannot be fulfilled by a guardian after death, contrary to Imam Shafi'i's view. Only the sin of omission remains on the deceased, subject to Allah's mercy or justice.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyah): Legal capacity to be obligated and to perform acts in this world.
حقوق الله (huquq Allah): Rights of Allah i.e. obligations of worship like prayer, fasting, zakah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is performing zakah on behalf of a deceased person invalid?
A) Because the deceased no longer owns the property.
B) Because zakah is an act of worship requiring volition, and the deceased cannot perform it.
C) Because only the state can collect zakah after death.
D) Because zakah is only valid during Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
Death completely removes legal capacity for acts of worship; obligations like zakah, prayer, and fasting cannot be fulfilled after death—only the sin of omission remains.

Need Persists After Death: Usul Principle and Applications
Explains that death does not negate need, applying to estate distribution, kitabah, and ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the usul principle 'al-hajah la tanfi bi'l-mawt' to derive rulings for estate distribution, kitabah, and ghusl after death.

Core Idea
Death does not extinguish legal need; the deceased's needs shape estate priorities and contractual obligations.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational usul principle: need (hajah) persists after death, influencing how we order the deceased's obligations and rights. The text demonstrates this by prioritising funeral expenses, debts, and bequests, then extends the principle to two specific cases—kitabah (manumission contract) and ghusl (washing the deceased). Understanding this principle helps you see how the Hanafi school derives rulings that balance the deceased's interests with those of heirs and other parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Extract the central statement: 'There remains for him that which fulfills his need' and note that death does not negate need. This is the usul rule being expanded.
The principle is stated at the beginning and repeated throughout.
2
Examine the Basis (Istidlal)
The principle is supported by both rational reasoning (dalil aqli) and a textual indication (dalil naqli) from the Prophet's hadith to Aisha. Identify how each supports the idea that need persists.
The hadith is interpreted indirectly: 'I would wash you' means arranging for washing, not performing it.
3
Apply to Estate Priorities
Follow the order: tajhiz (funeral), then debts, then bequests, then inheritance. Each step reflects a stronger or more immediate need. Understand why qisas is excluded.
The principle of need dictates that what fulfills the deceased's need is prioritised.
4
Apply to Kitabah and Ghusl
Analyse how the principle justifies the continuation of kitabah after the master's death (need for wala' and price) and after the mukatib's death (need for freedom). Then see how the wife's need to wash her husband during 'iddah is derived from the persistence of marital ownership.
Both applications show that need is not terminated by death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى له ما تقضى به الحاجة ولذالك قدم تجهيزه تم ديونه ثم وصاياه ثم وجب الميراث بطريق الخلافة عنه نظرا له فيصرف إلى من يصل به نسبا أو سببا أو دينا بلا نسب أو سبب يعني يوضع في بيت المال تقضى به حوائج المسلمين ولذلك أي ولان الموت لا ينافى الحاجة بقيت الكتابة بعد موت المولى وبعد موت المكاتب عن وفاء فإذا مات المولى وبقي المكاتب حيا يودي الكتابة إلى ورثته لاحتياج المولى إلى الولاء وبدل الكتابة وكذا إذا مات المكاتب عن وفاء أي مال واف لبدل الكتابة وبقي المولى حيا يودئ الوفاء ورثة المكاتب إلى المولى لحاجته على تحصيل الحرية حتى يكون ما بقي عنه ميرانا لورثته ويعتق أولاده المولودون والمستسرون في حال الكتابة ويعتق هو في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته وإنما قلنا عن وفاء لأنه إذا لم يترك وفاء لا ينبغي لأولاده أن يكسبوا الوفاء ويؤدوه إلى المولى وقلنا معطوف على قوله بقيت أي ولذلك قلنا تغسل المرأة زوجها في عدتها لبقاء ملك الزوج في العدة والمالك هو المحتاج إلى غسل بخلاف ما إذا ماتت المرأة حيث لا يغسلها زوجها لإنها مملوكة وقد بطلت يغسلها زوجها كما تغسل هي زوجها لقوله عليه السلام لعائشة لو مت لغسلتك والجواب أن معنى لغسلتك لقمت باسباب غسلك وما لا يصح لحاجته كالقصاص يحتمل أن يكون معطوفا على ما تقضى به الحاجة يعني بقي للميت ما تقضى به الحاجة وما لا يصح للحاجة كالقصاص ويحتمل أن يكون ابتداء كلام وقع مبتدأ وخبرا إنما أورده بتقريب ما تقضى به الحاجة وإنما يكون القصاص مما لا يصلح لحاجته فيصرف إلى من يصل به نسبا أو سببا أو دينا بلا نسب أو سبب يعني يوضع في بيت المال تقضى به حوائج المسلمين
There remains for him that which fulfills his need, and therefore his preparation (tajhiz) is prioritized, then his debts, then his bequests, then inheritance becomes obligatory by way of succession from him, as a consideration for him. It is then distributed to those connected by lineage (nasab) or reason (sabab) [marriage], or debt without lineage or reason – meaning it is placed in the treasury (bayt al-mal) to fulfill the needs of Muslims. And because death does not negate need, the kitabah (contract of manumission) remains after the death of the master and after the death of the mukatib (slave under kitabah) if he dies with sufficient funds. When the master dies and the mukatib lives, he pays the kitabah to the heirs of the master because the master needs the wala' (clientage) and the kitabah price. Likewise, if the mukatib dies with sufficient property to cover the kitabah, and the master lives, the heirs of the mukatib pay the master for his need to obtain freedom, so that what remains becomes inheritance for his heirs, and his children born or conceived during kitabah become free, and he becomes free in the last part of his life. We only say if he dies with sufficient funds because if he leaves insufficient funds, his children should not earn the payment and pay it to the master. And we say [this] is connected to His statement 'remains' – and therefore we say: a woman may wash her husband during her 'iddah because the husband's ownership remains during the 'iddah, and the owner is the one in need of washing. Contrary to when the woman dies, where her husband does not wash her because she is owned and ownership has ceased. The husband washes her as she washes him, due to his ﷺ saying to Aisha: 'If you died, I would wash you.' The answer is that the meaning of 'I would wash you' is 'I would undertake the means of your washing.' And what is not valid for need, like qisas (retaliation), may be connected to 'that which fulfills need' – meaning there remains for the deceased what fulfills his need, and what is not valid for need, like qisas. Or it may be an independent statement. He only mentioned it to approximate what fulfills need. Qisas is not among the things that are valid for his need. It is distributed to those connected by lineage, or reason, or debt without lineage or reason – meaning placed in the treasury to fulfill the needs of Muslims.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage establishes the usul rule that death does not negate legal need (hajah). The deceased's estate is first used for his funeral (tajhiz), then for debts, then bequests (within one-third), then inheritance. This order is based on the strength of need. The principle that need persists after death is then applied to two cases. First, kitabah (contractual manumission) continues after the death of either party. If the master dies, the mukatib must pay the remaining installments to the master's heirs because the master's need for wala' and the price remains. If the mukatib dies with sufficient funds, his heirs must pay the master to secure the mukatib's freedom, so that his free status is established at death and his children born during kitabah are free. Second, a wife may wash her deceased husband during her 'iddah because the husband's ownership (zawjiyyah) persists during that period, creating a need for her to perform the ghusl. Conversely, a husband does not wash his deceased wife because her ownership over herself terminates at death; however, the hadith 'if you died, I would wash you' is interpreted as 'I would arrange for your washing' not literally perform it. The passage clarifies that qisas (retaliation) does not qualify as a need for the deceased, so it is not included in the estate's priorities. The residual estate after debts and bequests goes to heirs by lineage or marriage, or if none, to the treasury for Muslim interests.

Key Terms
حاجة (hajah): Need that persists after death affecting distribution and legal relations
تجهيز (tajhiz): Preparation of the deceased (shroud, burial) funded from estate
كتابة (kitabah): Contract by which a slave purchases freedom by installments

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle in this lesson, why does kitabah continue after the master's death?
A) Because the slave needs to be free
B) Because the master's need for wala' and the kitabah price remains
C) Because death does not end contracts
D) Because the heirs inherit the slave

Key Takeaway
The usul principle 'al-hajah la tanfi bi'l-mawt' dictates that certain needs of the deceased survive death, shaping estate priorities and contractual obligations like kitabah.

8 min
The Principle of Need After Death: Kitabah as Proof
Commentary explaining that death does not negate need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that death does not negate need and apply it to explain why the kitabah contract survives after the death of either party.

Core Idea
Death ends life, but not the legal needs that justify the persistence of certain rights.

This passage introduces a foundational principle in Hanafi usul: need (ḥājah) persists beyond death, allowing certain legal rights to continue. The author uses the contract of kitabah as a clear example: even after the master or the slave dies (with sufficient funds), the contract remains enforceable because both parties still have unmet needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Locate the statement that death does not negate need: 'لأن الموت لا ينافي الحاجة'.
This is the core usul rule being illustrated.
2
Examine the kitabah application
After the master's death, the mukatab pays the heirs; after the mukatab's death (with enough property), his heirs pay the master. Both cases show the contract survives because the deceased's need for clientage (walā') or freedom persists.
The need is not personal physical need but legal interest.
3
Understand the condition of sufficiency
The mukatab must leave sufficient funds (ʿan wafā'). If not, his children are not obligated to earn and pay.
This limits the principle: need only justifies enforcement if the means are already present.
4
Generalize the principle
The same logic applies to other rights that survive death, such as debts and the wife's right to wash her husband during iddah.
This is an expansion probe: the principle is used to explain multiple fiqh rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيَصْرِفُ إِلى مَنْ يَأْصِلُ بِهِ نَسبَبًا أى قرابة أو سَبَبًا أَي زَوْجِيَةٌ أَوْ دَيْنًا بِلاَ نَسَبٍ أَوْ سَبَبٍ يَعْنِي يُوْضَعُ فِي بَيْتِ الْمَالِ تُقْضَى بِهِ حَوَائِجُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَلِهَذَا أى وَلاَنَّ الْمَوْت لايُنَافِى الْحَاجَةُ بَقِيَ الْكِتَابَةُ بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمَوْلى وَبَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمُكَاتَب عَنْ وَفَاءٍ فَإِذَا مَاتَ الْمَوْلَى وَبَقِيَ الْمُكَاتَبُ حَيًّا يُؤْدِى الْكِتَابَةُ إلَى وَرَثَتِهِ لاَحْتِيَاجِ الْمَوْلَى إِلَى الْوِلَاءِ وَبَدَلِ الْكِتَابَةِ وَكَذَا إِذَا مَاتَ الْمُكَاتَبُ عَنْ وَفَاءٍ أَى مَالٍ وَافٍ لِبَدْلِ الْكِتَابَةِ وَبَقِيَ الْمَوْلَى حَيًّا يُوَدِّئُ الْوَفَاءَ وَرَثَةُ الْمُكَاتَبِ إلى الْمَوْلَى لِحَاجَتِهِ عَلى تَحْصِيلِ الْحُرِّيَةِ حَتى يَكُونَ مَا بَقِيَ عَنْهُ مِيْرَانًا لَوَرَثَتِهِ وَيَعْتِق أَوْلاَدَة الْمَوْلُودَونَ وَالْمُسْتَرُونَ فِي حَالِ الْكِتَابَةِ وَيَعْتِقَ هُوَ فِي آخِرِ جُزءٍ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ حَيَاتِهِ وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا عَنْ وَفَاءِ لأَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَتْرُكَ وَفَاءً لَا يَنْبَغِي لِأَوْلَادِهِ أَنْ يَكْسِبُوا الْوَفَاءَ وَيَؤُدُّوهُ إِلَى الْمَوْلى وَقُلْنَا مَعْطَوْفٌ عَلى قَوْلِهِ بَقِيَتْ أَيْ وَلِهَذَا قُلْنَا تَغْسِلُ الْمَرْأةُ زَوْجَهَا فِي عِدَّتِهَا لِبَقَاءِ مِلْكِ الزَّوْجِ فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَالْمَالِكُ هُوَ الْمُحْتَاجُ إِلَى غُسْلِهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا مَاتَتِ الْمَرْأةُ حَيْثُ لَا يَغْسِلُهَا زَوْجُهَا لِأَنَّهَا مَمْلُوكَةٌ وَقَدْ بَطَلَتْ أَهْلِيَّةُ الْمَمْلُوكِيَّةِ بِالْمَوْتِ فَلَا يَغْسِلُهَا زَوْجُهَا كَمَا تَغْسِلُ هِيَ زَوْجَهَا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لِعَائِشَةَ لَوْ مُتِّ لَغَسَلْتُكِ وَالْجَوَابُ أَنَّ مَعْنَى لَغَسَلْتُكِ لَقُمْتُ بِأَسْبَابِ غُسْلِكِ وَمَالَا يَصْلُحُ لِحَاجَتِهِ كَالْقِصَاصِ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَعْطُوفًا عَلَى مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ يَعْنِي بَقَى لِلْمَيِّتِ مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَمَالَا يَصْلُحُ لِلْحَاجَةِ كَالْقِصَاصِ وَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ابْتِدَاءَ كَلَامٍ وَقَعَ مُبْتَدَأً وَخَيْرًا إِنَّمَا أَوْرَدَهُ بِتَقْرِيبِ مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَإِنَّمَا يَكُونُ الْقِصَاصُ مِمَّا لَا يَصْلُحُ لِحَاجَتِهِ لِأَنَّهُ شُرِعَ عُقُوبَةً لِدَرْكِ النَّارِ وَهُوَ تَشْفِيَةُ الصُّدُورِ لِلْأَوْلِيَاءِ بِدَفْعِ شَتَرِ الْقَاتِلِ وَوَقَعَتِ الْجِنَايَةُ عَلَى أَوْلِيَائِهِ مِنْ وَجْهِ انْتِفَاعِهِمْ بِحَيَاتِهِ فَأَوْجَنَّا الْقِصَاصَ لِلْوَرَثَةِ ابْتِدَاءً لَا أَنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ لِلْمَيِّتِ أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ يَنْتَقِلُ إِلَيْهِمْ كَالْحُقُوقِ وَالسَّبَبُ الْعَقْدُ لِلْمَيِّتِ [؟] لِأَنَّ الْمِثْلَ حَيَاتُهُ فَكَانَتِ الْجِنَايَةُ وَاقِعَةً فِي حَقِّهِ مِنْ وَجْهِ فَيَصِحُّ عَفْوُ [؟]
It is disbursed to those connected by lineage, i.e., kinship, or by cause, i.e., marriage, or by debt without lineage or cause, meaning it is placed in the treasury of Muslims, by which the needs of Muslims are fulfilled. And for this reason, because death does not negate need, the contract of kitabah remains after the death of the master and after the death of the mukatab who leaves sufficient payment. So when the master dies and the mukatab is alive, he pays the kitabah to the master's heirs due to the master's need for clientage and the kitabah payment. Likewise, when the mukatab dies leaving sufficient property to cover the kitabah payment and the master is alive, the heirs of the mukatab pay the payment to the master due to his need for completing freedom, so that what remains from him becomes inheritance for his heirs, and his children born or purchased during the kitabah are freed, and he himself is freed at the last moment of his life. And we only said 'leaving sufficient payment' because if he does not leave sufficient payment, it is not appropriate for his children to earn the payment and pay it to the master. And we said it is connected to his statement 'remained', i.e., and for this reason we said the woman washes her husband during her iddah because the husband's ownership remains during the iddah, and the owner is the one in need of his washing, contrary to when the woman dies, where her husband does not wash her because she was owned and the capacity of ownership is voided by death, so her husband does not wash her as she washes him, due to his statement, peace be upon him, to Aisha: 'If you died, I would wash you.' The answer is that the meaning of 'I would wash you' is 'I would undertake the means of your washing.' And what is not suitable for his need, like qisas, can be interpreted as connected to what fulfills need, meaning for the deceased remains what fulfills need and what is not suitable for need like qisas, and it can be interpreted as an independent statement occurring as subject and predicate. He only mentioned it due to proximity of what fulfills need, and qisas is among what is not suitable for his need, because it is prescribed as a punishment to avert the fire, and it is a solace for the heirs by removing the disgrace of the killer, and the injury occurred upon the heirs from the aspect of their benefiting from his life, so we made qisas for the heirs initially, not that it is established for the deceased first then transfers to them like other rights. And the cause is the contract for the deceased [؟] because the equivalent is his life, so the injury occurred regarding him from one aspect, so the pardon [of the guardian] is valid [؟].
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul commentary illustrating the principle that need (ḥājah) survives death. The author states that death does not negate need; therefore, certain rights and obligations persist after death. The first application is the distribution of the deceased's estate: it goes to those connected by lineage (relatives) or by cause (marriage), or if none, to the public treasury to fulfill the needs of Muslims. Then the commentary focuses on kitābah (contract of manumission): the contract remains valid after the death of either party—the master or the mukātab—provided the mukātab leaves sufficient funds. This is because the master needs the clientage (walā') and the payment, and the mukātab needs to achieve freedom so that his freed status benefits his heirs. If the mukātab leaves insufficient funds, his children are not obligated to earn money to pay; they are not required to work to fulfill the contract. Next, the author discusses the ghusl (washing) of spouses. According to the Hanafī school, a wife may wash her husband during her ʿiddah because the marriage ownership remains, making him in need of her washing. Conversely, if the wife dies, the husband cannot wash her because her status as owned (mamlūkah) is voided by death. The Shāfiʿī school argues that the husband can wash his wife based on the Prophet's statement to ʿĀ'ishah: 'If you died, I would wash you.' The Hanafī response interprets this as meaning the Prophet would arrange the washing, not perform it personally. Finally, the passage discusses qiṣāṣ (retaliation) as something that does not fulfill the deceased's need. Qiṣāṣ is a punishment for the killer to provide solace to the heirs and to avert divine punishment. It is established for the heirs directly, not for the deceased first, because it serves the living heirs' need for justice and closure. The deceased has no personal stake in it, as the purpose is punishment, not satisfaction of a need.

Key Terms
الحاجة (al-ḥājah): Need that persists after death, justifying the continuation of certain rights for the deceased's benefit (e.g., kitābah, ghusl).
الكتابة (al-kitābah): A contract of manumission where a slave agrees to pay a sum to the master in installments, upon which the slave becomes free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle described in this passage, why does the kitabah contract remain valid after the master's death?
A) Because the slave is still alive and must complete the payment.
B) Because the master's need for clientage and payment persists after death.
C) Because the contract is automatically renewed by operation of law.
D) Because the heirs force the slave to continue.

Key Takeaway
Death does not extinguish legal need; rights that fulfill a need can survive, as shown by the continuity of the kitabah contract.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Need After Death: The Ghusl Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on husband washing wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a husband cannot wash his deceased wife and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith.

Core Idea
The husband's ownership of the marriage ends at his death, but the wife's status as owned ends at hers—so only the wife can wash the husband.

This passage applies the principle of need after death to a specific inter-madhhab dispute: may a husband wash his deceased wife? The Hanafi school says no, because her status as owned (mamlūkah) ends with death, so there is no need for him to wash her. The Shafi'i school argues yes, based on the Prophet's statement to Aisha. The Hanafi response reinterprets the hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The wife may wash her husband during her iddah because his ownership remains; the husband may not wash his wife because her ownership capacity ends at death.
The key distinction is the direction of ownership.
2
Identify the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i school cites the hadith: 'If you died, I would wash you.' They take it literally as the Prophet performing the washing.
This hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school reinterprets the hadith: 'I would wash you' means 'I would undertake the means of your washing' (arranging, not doing).
This resolves the apparent contradiction with the principle of need.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, a husband cannot perform the actual washing; he must arrange it. In the Shafi'i school, he can do it himself.
This is a clear example of how usul principles affect fiqh rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَالْمُسْتَرُونَ فِي حَالِ الْكِتَابَةِ وَيَعْتِقَ هُوَ فِي آخِرِ جُزءٍ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ حَيَاتِهِ وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا عَنْ وَفَاءِ لأَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَتْرُكَ وَفَاءً لَا يَنْبَغِي لِأَوْلَادِهِ أَنْ يَكْسِبُوا الْوَفَاءَ وَيَؤُدُّوهُ إِلَى الْمَوْلى وَقُلْنَا مَعْطَوْفٌ عَلى قَوْلِهِ بَقِيَتْ أَيْ وَلِهَذَا قُلْنَا تَغْسِلُ الْمَرْأةُ زَوْجَهَا فِي عِدَّتِهَا لِبَقَاءِ مِلْكِ الزَّوْجِ فِي غُسْلِكِ وَمَالَا يَصْلُحُ لِحَاجَتِهِ كَالْقِصَاصِ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَعْطُوفًا عَلَى مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ يَعْنِي بَقَى لِلْمَيِّتِ مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَمَالَا يَصْلُحُ Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul commentary illustrating the principle that need (ḥājah) survives death. The author states that death does not negate need; therefore, certain rights and obligations persist after death. The first application is the distribution of the deceased's estate: it goes to those connected by lineage (relatives) or by cause (marriage), or if none, to the public treasury to fulfill the needs of Muslims. Then the commentary focuses on kitābah (contract of manumission): the contract remains valid after the death of either party—the master or the mukātab—provided the mukātab leaves sufficient funds. If the mukātab leaves insufficient funds, his children are not obligated to earn money to pay; they are not required to work to fulfill the contract. The deceased has no personal stake in it, as the purpose is punishment, not satisfaction of a need.

Key Terms
المملوكية (al-mamlūkiyyah): The state of being owned (as a slave or wife in terms of marital ownership); capacity that ends at death, affecting rulings like ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the passage, what does the Prophet's statement 'لَغَسَلْتُكِ' mean?
A) He would personally wash Aisha.
B) He would arrange for Aisha's washing.
C) He would pray for her.
D) He would wash her with his own hands.

Key Takeaway
The husband may not wash his deceased wife because her status as owned ends at death, and the hadith is interpreted as arranging the washing.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Qisas: A Right of the Heirs, Not a Need of the Deceased
Analysis of qisas as a right not based on need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rights that satisfy a deceased's need (like kitabah) and those that don't (like qisas), explaining why qisas is a direct right of the heirs.

Core Idea
Qisas is punishment for the heirs' solace, not a need of the deceased—so it never vests in the deceased.

This passage categorizes qisas (retaliation) as something that does not satisfy the deceased's need. While rights like kitabah and debts survive death because the deceased has a need for them, qisas serves a different purpose: it is a punishment that provides solace to the living heirs. Therefore, it belongs to the heirs directly, not transferred from the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the category
Identify that qisas is listed among 'what is not suitable for his need' (مَا لَا يَصْلُحُ لِحَاجَتِهِ).
The author contrasts it with what does fulfill need.
2
Understand the rationale
Qisas is prescribed as a punishment to avert the fire (of the Hereafter) and to give solace to the heirs by removing the disgrace of the killer. It benefits the living, not the deceased.
The purpose is not to satisfy a need of the deceased, but to provide justice to the heirs.
3
Trace the legal consequence
Because qisas is not a need of the deceased, it is established for the heirs directly (ابتداءً), not transferred to them from the deceased through inheritance.
This is different from debts or kitabah, which are first established for the deceased and then passed to heirs.
4
Contrast with need-based rights
Compare with kitabah: the deceased master needs clientage and payment, so those survive. For qisas, the deceased has no personal need for punishment; it is for the heirs.
This distinction helps classify various legal rights after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيَصْرِفُ إِلى مَنْ يَأْصِلُ بِهِ نَسبَبًا أى قرابة أو سَبَبًا أَي زَوْجِيَةٌ أَوْ دَيْنًا بِلاَ نَسَبٍ أَوْ سَبَبٍ يَعْنِي يُوْضَعُ فِي بَيْتِ الْمَالِ تُقْضَى بِهِ حَوَائِجُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَلِهَذَا أى وَلاَنَّ الْمَوْت لايُنَافِى الْحَاجَةُ بَقِيَ الْكِتَابَةُ بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمَوْلى وَبَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمُكَاتَب عَنْ وَفَاءٍ فَإِذَا مَاتَ الْمَوْلَى وَبَقِيَ الْمُكَاتَبُ حَيًّا يُؤْدِى الْعِدَّةِ وَالْمَالِكُ هُوَ الْمُحْتَاجُ إِلَى غُسْلِهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا مَاتَتِ الْمَرْأةُ حَيْثُ لَا يَغْسِلُهَا زَوْجُهَا لِأَنَّهَا مَمْلُوكَةٌ وَقَدْ بَطَلَتْ أَهْلِيَّةُ الْمَمْلُوكِيَّةِ بِالْمَوْتِ فَلَا يَغْسِلُهَا زَوْجُهَا كَمَا تَغْسِلُ هِيَ زَوْجَهَا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لِعَائِشَةَ لَوْ مُتِّ لَغَسَلْتُكِ وَالْجَوَابُ أَنَّ مَعْنَى لَغَسَلْتُكِ لَقُمْتُ بِأَسْبَابِ غُسْلِكِ وَمَالَا يَصْلُحُ لِحَاجَتِهِ كَالْقِصَاصِ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَعْطُوفًا عَلَى مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ يَعْنِي بَقَى لِلْمَيِّتِ مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَمَالَا يَصْلُحُ لِلْحَاجَةِ كَالْقِصَاصِ وَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ابْتِدَاءَ كَلَامٍ وَقَعَ مُبْتَدَأً وَخَيْرًا إِنَّمَا أَوْرَدَهُ بِتَقْرِيبِ مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَإِنَّمَا يَكُونُ الْقِصَاصُ مِمَّا لَا يَصْلُحُ لِحَاجَتِهِ لِأَنَّهُ شُرِعَ عُقُوبَةً لِدَرْكِ النَّارِ وَهُوَ تَشْفِيَةُ الصُّدُورِ لِلْأَوْلِيَاءِ بِدَفْعِ شَتَرِ الْقَاتِلِ وَوَقَعَتِ الْجِنَايَةُ عَلَى أَوْلِيَائِهِ مِنْ وَجْهِ انْتِفَاعِهِمْ بِحَيَاتِهِ فَأَوْجَنَّا الْقِصَاصَ لِلْوَرَثَةِ ابْتِدَاءً لَا أَنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ لِلْمَيِّتِ أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ يَنْتَقِلُ إِلَيْهِمْ كَالْحُقُوقِ وَالسَّبَبُ الْعَقْدُ لِلْمَيِّتِ [؟] لِأَنَّ الْمِثْلَ حَيَاتُهُ فَكَانَتِ الْجِنَايَةُ وَاقِعَةً فِي حَقِّهِ مِنْ وَجْهِ فَيَصِحُّ عَفْوُ [؟]
It is disbursed to those connected by lineage, i.e., kinship, or by cause, i.e., marriage, or by debt without lineage or cause, meaning it is placed in the treasury of Muslims, by which the needs of Muslims are fulfilled. And for this reason, because death does not negate need, the contract of kitabah remains after the death of the master and after the death of the mukatab who leaves sufficient payment. So when the master dies and the mukatab is alive, he pays the kitabah to the master's heirs due to the master's need for clientage and the kitabah payment. Likewise, when the mukatab dies leaving sufficient property to cover the kitabah payment and the master is alive, the heirs of the mukatab pay the payment to the master due to his need for completing freedom, so that what remains from him becomes inheritance for his heirs, and his children born or purchased during the kitabah are freed, and he himself is freed at the last moment of his life. And we only said 'leaving sufficient payment' because if he does not leave sufficient payment, it is not appropriate for his children to earn the payment and pay it to the master. And we said it is connected to his statement 'remained', i.e., and for this reason we said the woman washes her husband during her iddah because the husband's ownership remains during the iddah, and the owner is the one in need of his washing, contrary to when the woman dies, where her husband does not wash her because she was owned and the capacity of ownership is voided by death, so her husband does not wash her as she washes him, due to his statement, peace be upon him, to Aisha: 'If you died, I would wash you.' The answer is that the meaning of 'I would wash you' is 'I would undertake the means of your washing.' And what is not suitable for his need, like qisas, can be interpreted as connected to what fulfills need, meaning for the deceased remains what fulfills need and what is not suitable for need like qisas, and it can be interpreted as an independent statement occurring as subject and predicate. He only mentioned it due to proximity of what fulfills need, and qisas is among what is not suitable for his need, because it is prescribed as a punishment to avert the fire, and it is a solace for the heirs by removing the disgrace of the killer, and the injury occurred upon the heirs from the aspect of their benefiting from his life, so we made qisas for the heirs initially, not that it is established for the deceased first then transfers to them like other rights. And the cause is the contract for the deceased [؟] because the equivalent is his life, so the injury occurred regarding him from one aspect, so the pardon [of the guardian] is valid [؟].
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul commentary illustrating the principle that need (ḥājah) survives death. The author states that death does not negate need; therefore, certain rights and obligations persist after death. The first application is the distribution of the deceased's estate: it goes to those connected by lineage (relatives) or by cause (marriage), or if none, to the public treasury to fulfill the needs of Muslims. Then the commentary focuses on kitābah (contract of manumission): the contract remains valid after the death of either party—the master or the mukātab—provided the mukātab leaves sufficient funds. This is because the master needs the clientage (walā') and the payment, and the mukātab needs to achieve freedom so that his freed status benefits his heirs. If the mukātab leaves insufficient funds, his children are not obligated to earn money to pay; they are not required to work to fulfill the contract. Next, the author discusses the ghusl (washing) of spouses. According to the Hanafī school, a wife may wash her husband during her ʿiddah because the marriage ownership remains, making him in need of her washing. Conversely, if the wife dies, the husband cannot wash her because her status as owned (mamlūkah) is voided by death. The Shāfiʿī school argues that the husband can wash his wife based on the Prophet's statement to ʿĀ'ishah: 'If you died, I would wash you.' The Hanafī response interprets this as meaning the Prophet would arrange the washing, not perform it personally. Finally, the passage discusses qiṣāṣ (retaliation) as something that does not fulfill the deceased's need. Qiṣāṣ is a punishment for the killer to provide solace to the heirs and to avert divine punishment. It is established for the heirs directly, not for the deceased first, because it serves the living heirs' need for justice and closure. The deceased has no personal stake in it, as the purpose is punishment, not satisfaction of a need.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment for homicide or injury, prescribed as a right for the heirs or victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is qisas not considered a need of the deceased?
A) Because the deceased cannot benefit from punishment.
B) Because qisas is prescribed as a punishment and solace for the heirs.
C) Because qisas is forbidden in Islam.
D) Because the deceased's family must forgive.

Key Takeaway
Qisas is a direct right of the heirs because it serves as punishment and solace, not a need of the deceased.
